<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b1c4048b-9887-4edd-b6df-0850f317c2b8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1200166151" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1425524939784323446"><b>The Butcher Boy, Patrick McCabe</b><br/><br/>The Butcher Boy is a 1992 novel by Patrick McCabe. Set in a small town in Ireland in the early 1960's, it tells the story of Francis "Francie" Brady, a schoolboy who retreats into a violent fantasy world as his troubled home life collapses. <br/><br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سی و یکم ماه مارس سال 2014میلادی<br/><br/>عنوان: شاگرد قصّاب؛ نوشته: پاتریک مک کیب؛ مترجم: پیمان خاکسار؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، نشر چشمه، 1393، در 221ص، اندازه چهارده و نیم در بیست و یک سانتیمتر، داستانهای نویسندگان ایرلند</span>
  <span id="freeText1425524939784323446" style="display:none"><b>The Butcher Boy, Patrick McCabe</b><br /><br />The Butcher Boy is a 1992 novel by Patrick McCabe. Set in a small town in Ireland in the early 1960's, it tells the story of Francis "Francie" Brady, a schoolboy who retreats into a violent fantasy world as his troubled home life collapses. <br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سی و یکم ماه مارس سال 2014میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: شاگرد قصّاب؛ نوشته: پاتریک مک کیب؛ مترجم: پیمان خاکسار؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، نشر چشمه، 1393، در 221ص، اندازه چهارده و نیم در بیست و یک سانتیمتر، داستانهای نویسندگان ایرلندی - سده 20م<br /><br />مک‌.کیب این رمان را به صورت روایت اول شخص نوشته است؛ شخصیت اصلی این رمان نوجوان روان‌پریش و قاتلی به نام «فرنسی» است، که به دلیل همین روان‌پریشی، در روایتش هیچ مرزی بین واقعیت، با خیال و توهم، وجود ندارد؛ «فرنسی» هرچند جنایتکار است، اما حس همدلیِ خوانشگر را نیز برمی‌انگیزد؛ جناب آقای «پیمان خاکسار»، در بخشی از مقدمه ی خود، درباره ی ساختار و فرم نگارش این رمان، نوشته است «نکاتی را باید درباره ی ترجمه ی کتاب با شما در میان بگذارم؛ مک‌.کیب در «شاگرد قصاب» حداقل استفاده را از نشانه‌ های سجاوندی کرده، کتاب به هیچ عنوان علامت نقل قول ندارد، و خیلی جاها متن و گفتگو، بدون فاصله آمده، زبان انگلیسی این ظرفیت را داراست، که در آن متنی این‌ چنین را نوشت و نتیجه هم قابل خواندن باشد، ولی فارسی این‌گونه نیست؛ برای همین بعضی جاها از ویرگول (فقط ویرگول) استفاده کردم، تا جمله‌ های درهم، و بعضا طولانی کتاب، خوانشگر را نیازارد.» همین؛<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 22/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4623433046666815224"><br/>This is a powerful book, one that fills the reader with pity and terror. <br/><br/>The protagonist is Francie Brady, a sort of Irish Huckleberry Finn, with three important differences: 1) Francie still yearns to be respectable, 2) he has no wise Jim to guide him, and 3) he is despised by his town and betrayed by Joe, his own Tom Sawyer.   <span id="freeText4623433046666815224" style="display:none"><br />This is a powerful book, one that fills the reader with pity and terror. <br /><br />The protagonist is Francie Brady, a sort of Irish Huckleberry Finn, with three important differences: 1) Francie still yearns to be respectable, 2) he has no wise Jim to guide him, and 3) he is despised by his town and betrayed by Joe, his own Tom Sawyer. Alas, there is no Mississippi river to escape to, and the book ends in blood and madness. <br /><br />Yet--and this is one of the strange strengths of the book--it is narrated by Francie in an upbeat, almost jaunty tone that never loses its sense of humor or its humanity.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer177516563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10581202554763625718">The Butcher Boy or Francie Brady’s Descent Into <i>MADNESS</i> (<--imagine I’m using my best Dan Rather for 48 Hours Mystery voice there)<br/><br/>At first I thought this was going to be a sort of case study in the makings of a sociopath (failure to conform to social norms – check; deceitfulness – check; disregard for safety of others – check; consistent irresponsibility-check; check; check; check). But oh no, it is far far worse than that for our little Francie Brady. Francie Brady skips right through sociopath</span>
  <span id="freeText10581202554763625718" style="display:none">The Butcher Boy or Francie Brady’s Descent Into <i>MADNESS</i> (&lt;--imagine I’m using my best Dan Rather for 48 Hours Mystery voice there)<br /><br />At first I thought this was going to be a sort of case study in the makings of a sociopath (failure to conform to social norms – check; deceitfulness – check; disregard for safety of others – check; consistent irresponsibility-check; check; check; check). But oh no, it is far far worse than that for our little Francie Brady. Francie Brady skips right through sociopathy and lands smack dab in the middle of a complete psychotic break of epic proportions.<br /><br />Let me make a confession. I am not a crier. Books do not make me cry. I can only remember crying ONCE while reading The World According to Garp, when Garp’s son <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ dies in that tragic accident <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, remember that? Sigh. But here I am, sitting on the train making my way to work and suddenly this salty watery substance starts flowing from my eyes! What the hell McCabe? Why’d you have to go and make me cry? I’m reading the story and I’m thinking, “Why does he keep putting up fly paper? Where are all the flies coming from? And what’s with the smell? Pilchards? Is it rotten meat? What’s going on?” And then, DUN DUN! The officer says, “Maggots – they’re right through him,” and suddenly it dawns on me, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[HIS DAD HAS BEEN DEAD THIS WHOLE TIME! And he’s been living with him in the house. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> WHAT THE HECK!!!!.<br /><br />McCabe’s stream-of-consciousness writing style is difficult to adjust to at first. (Mr. McCabe, allow me to introduce you to quotation marks. And this here, this is a comma.) The story is told in such a way that you (the reader) aren’t sure whether what’s happening is actually happening, or if Francie is lost somewhere in his own imagination, or if he’s remembering something that happened in the past and mixing it all up with what’s happening in the present. Stick with it. It’ll start making sense. And although the content is often horrific, that Francie Brady is a funny mutha. <br /><br />Did you ever find yourself repeatedly wincing at the written word? Yeah, it’s one of those books. As I turned the last page, I found myself experiencing a serious case of cognitive dissonance. Because while Francie is undoubtedly a disturbed, cruel, reprehensible character, he had this sort of sarcastic wit that I really related to. I related to a psychopath. And I couldn't help feeling sorry for him. Fictional or no, I'm not sure how I feel about that.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10581202554763625718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_56670436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56670436" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56670436?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56670436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13844154521104412522">Previously with "Breakfast on Pluto" I got the "feel" for this Irish writer. Certainly, McCabe has his own brand. He loves his readers to fully embody his fully-realized protagonist; one enjoys the rational irrationalities that litter a wartorn broken psyche of Francie, a pauper who is taken in by the community only to be persecuted for a hideous crime. Think of an Irish Charley from "Flowers for Algernon."<br/><br/>Think too, "A Clockwork Orange"! The ultraviolence & a masterful, invented dialogue is als</span>
  <span id="freeText13844154521104412522" style="display:none">Previously with "Breakfast on Pluto" I got the "feel" for this Irish writer. Certainly, McCabe has his own brand. He loves his readers to fully embody his fully-realized protagonist; one enjoys the rational irrationalities that litter a wartorn broken psyche of Francie, a pauper who is taken in by the community only to be persecuted for a hideous crime. Think of an Irish Charley from "Flowers for Algernon."<br /><br />Think too, "A Clockwork Orange"! The ultraviolence &amp; a masterful, invented dialogue is also present here. Francie is a psychopath (a-la "American Psycho", a-la "Zombie" by Joyce Carol Oates) who becomes the Butcher boy, or apprentice, killing with an underrated joy the pigs and piglets of the sty. Despite what is in his gray matter, his hands do things independently. Is he possessed? Is he the "bi-product of his environment"? Maybe. Yes. He's truly insane, and to actually go deep into the abysmal terrors, like a marvelous case study, is what McCabe excels at. It is also poignant and funny. Like a twisted Huck Finn, a macabre Holden Caufield, an astute Oliver Twist. Francie is as unforgettable as all those winnies... &amp; the patina he gives off, of a sad, pathetic underdog, perennial lost child, gives him immediate candidacy to the literary equivalent of Our Patron Saint of Unadulterated Evil.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13844154521104412522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_558986499" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558986499" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558986499?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=booker-long-and-shortlist">booker-long-and-shortlist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=irish-literature">irish-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558986499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17274878617524555756">Patrick McCabe perfectly captures the voice of Francie Brady, who remembers his childhood a young derelict somewhere in rural Ireland during the early 1960's in this violent, surreal but also moving book. Francie's voice carries the story, which is compelling, hilariously campy and absolutely tragic at the same time - we laugh only because we don't want to cry.<br/><br/>The novel's biggest success is its duality - the total immersion in Francie's mind, and its refusal - or inability - to perceive and unde</span>
  <span id="freeText17274878617524555756" style="display:none">Patrick McCabe perfectly captures the voice of Francie Brady, who remembers his childhood a young derelict somewhere in rural Ireland during the early 1960's in this violent, surreal but also moving book. Francie's voice carries the story, which is compelling, hilariously campy and absolutely tragic at the same time - we laugh only because we don't want to cry.<br /><br />The novel's biggest success is its duality - the total immersion in Francie's mind, and its refusal - or inability - to perceive and understand certain things, and us being aware of what's happening outside his bubble. In his naivete, Francie always has a justification and explanation for any event or situation, even if it's absolutely absurd - a fan of comic book and movies with John Wayne, he thinks in terms of popular culture of the era and acts accordingly. Francie is a character who's often difficult to sympathize with - he's cruel to a new schoolboy, Philip Nugent, bullies him and takes away his comic collection, provoking a feud with Nugent's mother which will spark the book and eventually prove tragic. Yes only the coldest heart would not feel compassion for him, even when he's at his most revolting - behind this skin and these eyes there still lies a yearning for childhood innocence, forever lost so long ago amidst apathy, emotional and physical violence and the endless petty cruelties. Little by little it was jabbed away by pins and needless of small-town meanness until the flesh was raw and vulnerable; perhaps in a different time and different place Francie Brady would be a completely different person, but in this incarnation he is Francie Brady, The Butcher Boy.<br /><br />Ah, Francie! Ah, humanity!<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="17274878617524555756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_266518234" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/266518234" />
    <a title="Ben Winch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5647943-ben-winch"><img alt="Ben Winch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605154548p2/5647943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/266518234?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben Winch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben Winch" href="/user/show/5647943-ben-winch">Ben Winch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/1056737.The_Butcher_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=irish">irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=anglo">anglo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer266518234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10207222302598665903"><blockquote>When I was a young lad twenty or thirty or forty years ago I lived in a town where they were all after me on account of what I done on Mrs Nugent.</blockquote><br/>From memory, this is the first sentence of <i>The Butcher Boy</i>, a book that meant a lot to me fifteen or twenty years ago when I was a young man and lived in a town in the Adelaide Hills. Just the fact that I remember the line proves the book affected me; I don't remember many first lines but for all-time classics like 'Somebody must have been telling lies</span>
  <span id="freeText10207222302598665903" style="display:none"><blockquote>When I was a young lad twenty or thirty or forty years ago I lived in a town where they were all after me on account of what I done on Mrs Nugent.</blockquote><br />From memory, this is the first sentence of <i>The Butcher Boy</i>, a book that meant a lot to me fifteen or twenty years ago when I was a young man and lived in a town in the Adelaide Hills. Just the fact that I remember the line proves the book affected me; I don't remember many first lines but for all-time classics like 'Somebody must have been telling lies about Joseph K...' or 'I owe the discovery of Uqbar to the conjunction of a mirror and an encyclopaedia'; I don't even remember the first line of Hamsun's <i>Mysteries</i>, for Christ's sake! So it's clear to me I liked <i>The Butcher Boy</i>, though I should put this in context: I was twenty or twenty-one when I read it, and just on the cusp of growing into maturity as a reader, which is to say, of defining what <i>I</i> loved most in literature, as opposed to what school or critics or my parents had suggested to me. Not only that, but I'd just had an artistic epiphany through film - via <i>Clockwork Orange</i> - which had left me especially receptive to a first-person, slang-infused, punctuation-defying account of the life of a young thug and murderer. 'A cross between Huck Finn and Hannibal Lector,' is how the back-cover blurb described protagonist Francie Brady, a kid in rural Ireland in the sixties with an alcoholic/musician/layabout father and a repressed-romantic mother who is in and out of the asylum (or 'garage', as Francie would have it) as often as her husband is found face-down in the pub, and a best friend, Joe, who drops him like a hot potato as both boys reach their teens and it becomes clear - to Joe if not Francie - that they're destined for very different futures. It's a sad, sad story - a melodrama - but told with such wit and irony that it becomes a demented black-humorous fantasia. As I say, it's a while since I've read it, and it may be that its technique would seem too obvious or flashy to me now, but I remember being delighted by the <i>duality</i> of everything Francie tells us: for instance the scene in which he takes a crap on Mrs Nugent's (his neighbour's, and arch enemy's) loungeroom floor, all the time invoking some duel between Captain Nemo and an octopus which somehow results in this huge 'submarine' ending up on the carpet. When he writes 'PIGS' in shit on the wall he's got some heroic context/justification for that too, and this child-psychopath's vision is actually sustained for the whole book. It's hilarious, brutal, scary, and nothing like the film. (How could it be? To my knowledge this 'dual perception' just isn't possible in film.) The only problem is, if you read anything else (or anything else that I'm aware of) by McCabe it only lessens the effect. The first book, <i>Carn</i>, was OK, but the third one <i>The Dead School</i> was, for the few pages I endured, a pale shadow of its predecessor, as was any fragment I took a look at thereafter. For this reason, <i>The Butcher Boy</i> gives the impression of having gestated over years, of having required and benefitted from more work than anything McCabe would write once it propelled him to the lower strata of the bigtime. And for all my qualifications, I'm pretty sure it still stands up, only not quite so sure that it's as intrinsically <i>mine</i> as I thought it was back then. Francie Brady, man! He's one of the great characters - a teenage Don Quixote with a comic-book fixation and an anger-management problem. He'll break your heart.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10207222302598665903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_401953280" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/401953280" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/401953280?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2008-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=irish">irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=crime">crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer401953280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4587120706025209005">A story about a boy, <b>Francie Brady</b> who becomes a criminal simply because of the situation he finds himself in. His father is a drunkard and his battered mother has a suicidal tendency. He has a good friend, like Tom is to Huck, <b>Joe</b> but one day they steal the comics collection of another boy, <b>Phillip</b> and the boy's mother is so angry she calls Francie's family PIGS. Feeling disappointed with how his father treated his uncle <b>Alo</b> who he admires, Francie goes to Dublin and becomes a petty thief. When</span>
  <span id="freeText4587120706025209005" style="display:none">A story about a boy, <b>Francie Brady</b> who becomes a criminal simply because of the situation he finds himself in. His father is a drunkard and his battered mother has a suicidal tendency. He has a good friend, like Tom is to Huck, <b>Joe</b> but one day they steal the comics collection of another boy, <b>Phillip</b> and the boy's mother is so angry she calls Francie's family PIGS. Feeling disappointed with how his father treated his uncle <b>Alo</b> who he admires, Francie goes to Dublin and becomes a petty thief. When he comes back, he loses his Joe to Phillip and so he takes revenge and that is the start of his path towards becoming a certified psychopath.<br /><br />It is a story of a boy's descent to madness. Told in the first-person narrative and in a charming Irish English kind of way, I was reminded of Frank McCourt's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/252577.Angela_s_Ashes" title="Angela's Ashes by Frank McCourt" rel="nofollow noopener">Angela's Ashes</a> (4 stars) except that the things that Francie says are mostly dark and disgusting. There were some funny and okay, innocent in a way young Irish boys I supposed think and talk, but while reading I could not follow some of Francie's thoughts unlike when I was reading McCourt's. I guess the mind of Francie was too dark for my brain to handle. <br /><br /><i>The Butcher Boy</i> is written without punctuation marks and separation of dialogues and thoughts that I found challenging yet it seemed to me that McCabe probably wanted to capture in this style what goes on in the mind of a criminal boy. So, there is no point in comparing this with the young McCourt whose main problems are how to get food for himself and his family and a cure for his red eyes. Here the young Francie does all those crimes while working in an abattoir as a butcher because deep inside he is longing for acceptance and love not only from his parents but also from his friend, Joe. <br /><br />So, like Nabokov's <i>Lolita,</i> this book may have a disgusting and disturbing storyline but if you dig deeper this is just a story of a boy who wants to make his parents proud of him and feel loved and wanted. That's where <i>Lolita's</i> magic lies and that's also the afterthought I had after reading this whole book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4587120706025209005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1221132379" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1221132379" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/275498-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418942629p2/275498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1221132379?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/275498-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780330323581" href="/book/show/1368628.The_Butcher_Boy">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jim by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/14258634-sarah">SARAH</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275498-jim?shelf=craziness">craziness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275498-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275498-jim?shelf=1001-bymrbyd">1001-bymrbyd</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275498-jim?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275498-jim?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275498-jim?shelf=gr-friend-recommended">gr-friend-recommended</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1221132379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18013956954196072831">This book seemed to me to be an amalgamation of <i>Psycho</i> and <i>Fatal Attraction</i>. We are introduced to Francie, an Irish lad, and are held as a captive audience as he descends into madness. Except that he doesn't really descend; it might to be more appropriate to say he sidesteps into madness...he was pretty close to the starting point when he was at his best.<br/><br/>Francie is the product of a drunken, abusive, layabout father and a submissive and suicidal mother. He has few friends, only one really, but he</span>
  <span id="freeText18013956954196072831" style="display:none">This book seemed to me to be an amalgamation of <i>Psycho</i> and <i>Fatal Attraction</i>. We are introduced to Francie, an Irish lad, and are held as a captive audience as he descends into madness. Except that he doesn't really descend; it might to be more appropriate to say he sidesteps into madness...he was pretty close to the starting point when he was at his best.<br /><br />Francie is the product of a drunken, abusive, layabout father and a submissive and suicidal mother. He has few friends, only one really, but he likes that friend a lot...to the point of jealousy and obsession. He wants to stay friends forever and simply cannot comprehend that the object of his affection might want to move on or, God forbid, have other friends. This leads him to commit a number of acts that require him to be institutionalized. On release, he is more damaged than he was when he went in. As in Yeats' poem, things fall apart in spite of Francie's efforts to stay straight. The ending is predictably and inevitably tragic.<br /><br />Although there is a realistic body count in this book, this book isn't strictly a gorefest. It is a story of loyalty, obsession, rejection, and a pretty accurate depiction of the manner in which people needing help are let down by the system.<br /><br />I count this as a horror story, but Francie isn't a monster, at least not in the way we think of monsters. The horrific part is that there are thousands of Francies in the making right now in every country in the world; always have been, always will be.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18013956954196072831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_97911610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97911610" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97911610?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780880641470" href="/book/show/138969.The_Butcher_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late">20th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=1001-books-list">1001-books-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97911610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13574949890889982954">There are certain books I've read that are so intense that they have left an indelible print on me, despite what I've thought of the book overall. Over the years that list has grown and include Jerzy Kosinksi's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18452.The_Painted_Bird' title='The Painted Bird by Jerzy Kosiński' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Painted Bird</a>, Bret Easton Ellis's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho' title='American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis' rel='nofollow noopener'>American Psycho</a>, Iain Banks's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory' title='The Wasp Factory by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wasp Factory</a>, and Anthony Burgess's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange' title='A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Clockwork Orange</a>. I can now add Patrick McCabe's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82965.The_Butcher_Boy' title='The Butcher Boy by Patrick McCabe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Butcher Boy</a> to that list.<br/><br/>This first-person stream-of-consciousness narrative takes some getting used to. Francie Brady reflect</span>
  <span id="freeText13574949890889982954" style="display:none">There are certain books I've read that are so intense that they have left an indelible print on me, despite what I've thought of the book overall. Over the years that list has grown and include Jerzy Kosinksi's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18452.The_Painted_Bird" title="The Painted Bird by Jerzy Kosiński" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Bird</a>, Bret Easton Ellis's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho" title="American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">American Psycho</a>, Iain Banks's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a>, and Anthony Burgess's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a>. I can now add Patrick McCabe's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82965.The_Butcher_Boy" title="The Butcher Boy by Patrick McCabe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Butcher Boy</a> to that list.<br /><br />This first-person stream-of-consciousness narrative takes some getting used to. Francie Brady reflects on his childhood living at home with his abusive and alcoholic father, and his beaten and suicidal mother. Francie and his best friend, Joe, make friends with Francie's neighbor, Phillip Nugent. The new friendship doesn't last long, however, and Phillip's mother lashes out at the Brady family, accusing them of being "pigs". Francie takes on this persona in some ways, embraces the status of a pig, and actually uses the Nugent's house at one point as a pig-sty. When Francie is caught in this act of behaving as swine, he is sent away to be educated by priests... just pick up a newspaper today and you'll see accusations of similar abuse inflicted upon Francie in this school. His life continues a downward spiral, and after leaving the school and returning home to work in a slaughterhouse, but finds his best friend has grown apart from him, and it all becomes too much for Francie. He uses the skills he has learned in the slaughterhouse to make his point in a rather graphic way.<br /><br />As a reader, I felt for Francie. Like Alex in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a>, Francie has no chance. It's arguable that the society in which he lives provides him no alternatives. Francie comes from an abusive family only to be put in an abusive environment allowing figures of authority molest him. You want to hug this kid. But then he opens his mouth and you realize instead of hugging him you really want to slug him. And you think he probably had it all coming, and he deserves everything that happens to him.<br /><br />And then you realize you are just a member of society.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13574949890889982954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_735888467" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/735888467" />
    <a title="Lynda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13400128-lynda"><img alt="Lynda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558931714p2/13400128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/735888467?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynda" href="/user/show/13400128-lynda">Lynda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition 9781447230748" href="/book/show/18632642-the-butcher-boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=z-read-2013">z-read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=ebooks-amazon">ebooks-amazon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=author-ireland">author-ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer735888467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8719708083427892331"><b>
  <i>"When I was a young lad twenty or thirty or forty years ago I lived in a small town where they were all after me on account of what I done on Mrs Nugent".</i>
</b><br/><br/><b>Synopsis</b><br/><br/>The Butcher Boy is a darkly comic yet disturbing novel by Irish author Patrick McCabe. Set in a small town in Ireland in the early 1960s, it tells the story of Francis "Francie" Brady, a 12 year old boy who retreats into a violent fantasy world to escape the reality of his dysfunctional family. Francie's father is an ex-musician and</span>
  <span id="freeText8719708083427892331" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"When I was a young lad twenty or thirty or forty years ago I lived in a small town where they were all after me on account of what I done on Mrs Nugent".</i>
</b><br><br><b>Synopsis</b><br><br>The Butcher Boy is a darkly comic yet disturbing novel by Irish author Patrick McCabe. Set in a small town in Ireland in the early 1960s, it tells the story of Francis "Francie" Brady, a 12 year old boy who retreats into a violent fantasy world to escape the reality of his dysfunctional family. Francie's father is an ex-musician and a drunk and his mother is mentally unstable and spends time in a sanatorium. Francie immerses himself in comic books and John Wayne movies to cope. He sees his world begin to fall apart on the day that a neighbour, Mrs Nugent, denounces him and his family as pigs. She then becomes a fixture in Francie's imagination. Circumstances spiral out of control at this point, his sanity deteriorates and he begins acting out his fantasies with increasing brutality and gruesome revenge. <br><br><b>Author</b><br><br>When I read these types of books, I do find myself contemplating the state of mind of the author and in McCabe's case, his absorption into a disordered mind and the blurring of the real and the imagined; the madness and the sanity (whatever that may be!). It wasn't until I did a little research on the author that I discovered some striking parallels between his own life and that of Francie.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1381516726p8/863689.jpg" width="200" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Patrick Joseph McCabe was born on March 27 1955 in the small country town of Clones, County Monaghan. The home life in the McCabe household was less than tranquil. His father had a drink problem and moved between jobs, all the while harbouring an ambition to become a musician and songwriter. <br><br> <i>"He was a very fine trumpet player"</i>, says McCabe, <i>"with a very good knowledge of music, and very well read. Like a lot of that generation, he was poorly schooled but highly educated."</i><br><br>His mother also possessed a fiery, self-tutored intelligence that made for a dangerous marriage; two people who were in competition rather than complementing each other. <br><br>McCabe escaped from this conflict through the world of comics. <br><br> <i>"There were all these different worlds, and the Irish world of daily reality was kind of mixed up with the comics in my mind."</i> <br><br>Even now, he still maintains an impressive comic collection. <br><br>Entertaining vague thoughts of becoming a writer, he headed off for Dublin to study, enrolling at St. Patrick's Teacher Training College, and soon became immersed in Dublin's nascent hippy scene. Being a hippy in 70s Dublin involved consuming considerable amounts of hallucinogenic drugs!<br><br>For approx 20 years McCabe taught at various schools, wrote poetry, short stories and radio plays. His first full-length published works: a children's story called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/698358.The_Adventures_of_Shay_Mouse_The_Mouse_from_Longford" title="The Adventures of Shay Mouse The Mouse from Longford by Patrick McCabe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventures of Shay Mouse</a> (1985), was illustrated by his wife, painter Margot Quinn. This was followed a year later by the novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/591338.Music_on_Clinton_Street" title="Music on Clinton Street by Patrick McCabe" rel="nofollow noopener">Music on Clinton Street</a>.<br><br>In 1992, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82965.The_Butcher_Boy" title="The Butcher Boy by Patrick McCabe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Butcher Boy</a> was published. It won the 1992 Irish Times Irish Literature Prize for Fiction and was shortlisted for the 1992 Booker Prize, catapulting McCabe from almost total obscurity into the literary limelight. McCabe was shortlisted again for the 1998 Booker Prize for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/782171.Breakfast_on_Pluto" title="Breakfast on Pluto by Patrick McCabe" rel="nofollow noopener">Breakfast on Pluto</a>, another twisted novel about a down-and-out Irish glam transvestite prostitute (next on my To Read list!). McCabe is now considered one of Ireland's foremost contemporary writers, coining his own genre <b>"Bog Gothic"</b> because of the macabre and gruesome content of his novels and his preferred setting of small town, rural Ireland.<br><br><b>Review</b><br><br>The Butcher Boy is similar in some respects to Iain Banks' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a> which I read and reviewed in August. Both are narrated by troubled juvenile boys from dysfunctional families and whose actions are driven by emotional poverty; a tragic lack of love and hope. Authors like McCabe and Banks have an incredible talent for creating a compassion for the narrator even as he shocks and revolts us.<br><br>I loved The Butcher Boy. What I particularly enjoyed was the deliberate dearth of punctuation which called attention to the sing-song hysteria of Francie. It gave it immediacy and honesty; the innocence of youth so startlingly pure, spoiled.<br><br>I have wondered (and at times worried about) why I am drawn to these deeply dark, twisted, violent and disturbing books. I think ultimately it is because of the adenaline infused emotion of being placed right smack inside of the warped mind of the protagonist while at the same time being aware of what is going on outside of the delusion and ignorance. It is a ride like no other!<br><br>Irrespective of genre, a good book is one that lives with you and never fully goes away. It leaves an imprint on your consciousness, for whatever reason. This book will remain with me for some time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8719708083427892331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1246238502" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1246238502" />
    <a title="Sonja Arlow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6449756-sonja-arlow"><img alt="Sonja Arlow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576564309p2/6449756.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1246238502?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sonja Arlow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sonja Arlow" href="/user/show/6449756-sonja-arlow">Sonja Arlow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6449756-sonja-arlow?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6449756-sonja-arlow?shelf=creepy">creepy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1246238502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12228368654491728505"><i>“Francie Brady is a disaffected, working-class, Roman Catholic teenager living in Northern Ireland. His alcoholic father works in the local slaughterhouse and his mother, despite being a whir of household efficiency, is suicidal.” </i><br/><br/>Butcher Boy traces the psychological workings of this young boy who turns into a murderer.<br/><br/>The lack of chapters and punctuation combined with the fact that Francie is clearly an unreliable narrator gave this an instant feeling of creepy confusion. At no point did I enjo</span>
  <span id="freeText12228368654491728505" style="display:none"><i>“Francie Brady is a disaffected, working-class, Roman Catholic teenager living in Northern Ireland. His alcoholic father works in the local slaughterhouse and his mother, despite being a whir of household efficiency, is suicidal.” </i><br /><br />Butcher Boy traces the psychological workings of this young boy who turns into a murderer.<br /><br />The lack of chapters and punctuation combined with the fact that Francie is clearly an unreliable narrator gave this an instant feeling of creepy confusion. At no point did I enjoy the story but I cannot deny that it was a most unusual experience that will stay with me for a long time.<br /><br />Another reviewer called it, claustrophobic narrative, which is a perfect description of how reading this made me feel. You are inside Francie’s head and he goes off the rails slowly but surely and you are stuck for the ride whether you like it or not. <br /><br />I should really be giving this a 4 star rating because I think the writing deserves it but to be honest my own personal experience makes it difficult to justify a higher rating. The author does a disturbingly good job of getting into Francie’s convoluted mind but more than once I had to stop and ask myself if I actually wanted to finish this book.<br /><br />I heaved a sigh of relief I finally finished this novel and if you are fine with books that make you feel uncomfortable and a storyline that deals with quite disturbing and disgusting subject matter then this book is for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12228368654491728505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2446076422" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2446076422" />
    <a title="Zak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68295640-zak"><img alt="Zak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500708503p2/68295640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2446076422?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zak" href="/user/show/68295640-zak">Zak</a>

  <span id="freeText5836105736674756077" style="display:none">i thoroughly enjoyed the writing style, as it reminded me in subtle ways of Samuel Beckett... i found the plot a bit lacking though... the meanderings and musings and mental machinations of the titular character are funny and sad and downright disturbing... there is a great feel for the small town here, and the characters are all, well, quite the characters... <div id="review_3012395760" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3012395760" />
    <a title="Rob Twinem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3542870-rob-twinem"><img alt="Rob Twinem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542181638p2/3542870.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3012395760?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob Twinem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob Twinem" href="/user/show/3542870-rob-twinem">Rob Twinem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3012395760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3753919462569116574">Last year the Booker prize winner was Milkman by Anna Burns and what a disappointing read that proved to be. Set in Belfast this was a book of gossip told in the first person by "middle sister" in a very claustrophobic and confusing style. Why should I tell you this? quite simply it is only to draw a comparison between a book that did not deserve the prize and a book published in 1992 that was Booker shortlisted but did not win....and what a pity it didn't......<br/><br/> The Butcher Boy is a highly enter</span>
  <span id="freeText3753919462569116574" style="display:none">Last year the Booker prize winner was Milkman by Anna Burns and what a disappointing read that proved to be. Set in Belfast this was a book of gossip told in the first person by "middle sister" in a very claustrophobic and confusing style. Why should I tell you this? quite simply it is only to draw a comparison between a book that did not deserve the prize and a book published in 1992 that was Booker shortlisted but did not win....and what a pity it didn't......<br /><br /> The Butcher Boy is a highly entertaining tour de force novel set in a small Irish Village. The prose is direct and very similar in style to The Wasp Factory by Ian Banks where the main character is also the narrator. In The Butcher Boy our narrator is Francie Brady probably best described as a bit of a scallywag, a good-for-nothing who with his best friend Joe spend their days in a carefree way more an inconvenience to the residents than a real threat. When they make the acquaintance of a local lad Philip Nugent and act in a somewhat dishonest way, refusing to return his comics, Mrs Nugent steps in to rescue the situation and in so doing changes the life of not only Francie but indirectly hers which is only revealed when the book concludes.<br /><br /> This is a startling novel giving great insight into life in a small Irish community in the early 1960s. Through the eyes of Francie we soon become acquainted with the somewhat joie de vivre attitude of the locals none more accommodating than Brady himself. However the second part of the novel displays a bitter and disturbing series of events; events that will have a long reaching affect on not only our narrator but an unsuspecting target. What starts off as a jovial account suddenly changes direction with impromptu violence and a very dark ending, with echoes of Charles Manson. Along the way it is easy to see how it only takes a little act to upset the balance of peace and how such an act can have deadly consequences...nothing is what it seems, people are not what they seem...With a very snappy dialogue that crackles along the book deserves to be read in one sitting..."He had a big breeze block of a head and a pair of eyebrows like two slugs trying to stand up"...."it was funny that face it slowly grew over the other one until one day you looked and the person you knew was gone."....."Oh ma I said the whole house is burning up on us then a fist made of smoke hit me a smack in the mouth its over says ma its all over now".....Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3753919462569116574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45764219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1050529390887602857">The Butcher Boy is one of those amazing books that you hope won't end. I read other reviews in which people said that it is depressing, and yes it is if you look at the plot outline of a mentally disturbed boy who doesn't have the best of luck. However there is a lot of dark humour in it that has you giggling despite yourself. The plot is dark, there are some very disturbing issues like Paedophilia, suicide etc... but by showing it through the eyes of a mad boy these topics are not touched in a </span>
  <span id="freeText1050529390887602857" style="display:none">The Butcher Boy is one of those amazing books that you hope won't end. I read other reviews in which people said that it is depressing, and yes it is if you look at the plot outline of a mentally disturbed boy who doesn't have the best of luck. However there is a lot of dark humour in it that has you giggling despite yourself. The plot is dark, there are some very disturbing issues like Paedophilia, suicide etc... but by showing it through the eyes of a mad boy these topics are not touched in a serious manner, you can happily read through giggling at the genius of Patrick McCabe. I would recommend it to anyone who does not get shocked easily and has good imagination and a dark sense of humour.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1050529390887602857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1025347582" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1025347582" />
    <a title="Joey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10049704-joey"><img alt="Joey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493309397p2/10049704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1025347582?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey" href="/user/show/10049704-joey">Joey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10049704-joey?shelf=fictions">fictions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10049704-joey?shelf=1001-best-novels-you-must-read">1001-best-novels-you-must-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1025347582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15153871401467854226">You might cringe and boggle at it if you give it a try. The novel is written with very little punctuation without separating the dialogs and thoughts. I could not even follow the exact settings. That is why I put it aside for almost one month. Perhaps I am not used to this kind of writing style. In the end, I have gotten around to it. I have teased its essence out. The writing style is the mental state of the protagonist. Also, the content of the book has satirical meanings.<br/><br/>Amidst the intricate </span>
  <span id="freeText15153871401467854226" style="display:none">You might cringe and boggle at it if you give it a try. The novel is written with very little punctuation without separating the dialogs and thoughts. I could not even follow the exact settings. That is why I put it aside for almost one month. Perhaps I am not used to this kind of writing style. In the end, I have gotten around to it. I have teased its essence out. The writing style is the mental state of the protagonist. Also, the content of the book has satirical meanings.<br /><br />Amidst the intricate narration, it occurred to me that Patrick McCabe wants to illustrate how a child, unloved, a victim of a broken family for his mother is verbally and physically abused by his sardonic alcoholic father and has suicidal tendency and committed for a mental institute, molested by a priest, ratted on by his only best friend, Joe Purcell, perceives his little world. In the end Francie becomes a psychopath killing Mrs. Nugget. <br /><br />In the context of satire, the psychological instability of Francie Brady has something to do with the socio-political state of Ireland during the sixties. At that time, there was rapid change as well as ethnic and political violence within Ireland, which is the responsible for molding a dysfunctional family. In addition, the novel alludes to the TOLL TAX, the moral status of the church, IRA, and what not. Only an Irish or World History scholar could best analyze it literally. <br /><br />Although the book was intentionally written for Ireland, so I could not completely relate to its real socio-political history, the novel is still engrossing. Poor Francie. He bled my heart. If I were Irish, I would give it 5 stars. <br /><br />If I had long vacation, or reached my retirement age, it would be one of the books I would give a try again. Why not? I liked the creative idea of Patrick McCabe. ^^<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15153871401467854226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26249479" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26249479" />
    <a title="Spudsie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1296189-spudsie"><img alt="Spudsie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1215572268p2/1296189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26249479?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Spudsie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Spudsie" href="/user/show/1296189-spudsie">Spudsie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Spudsie by: </span>
      2007 SRL
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26249479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5658819664555110624">Typically I try to record how a book leaves me feeling, any lessons I may have learned from it or even my overall mood when I’m finished. This may turn out to be my shortest commentary to date.<br/><br/>Confusion.<br/><br/>Yup. I feel so lost. Confused. Puzzled. ?????<br/><br/>When I read books I want the story and characters to unfold and reveal themselves to me as the author intended. To that end I try not to read any reviews or commentaries about a book right before I start it. When I’m finished reading it I try to put my</span>
  <span id="freeText5658819664555110624" style="display:none">Typically I try to record how a book leaves me feeling, any lessons I may have learned from it or even my overall mood when I’m finished. This may turn out to be my shortest commentary to date.<br /><br />Confusion.<br /><br />Yup. I feel so lost. Confused. Puzzled. ?????<br /><br />When I read books I want the story and characters to unfold and reveal themselves to me as the author intended. To that end I try not to read any reviews or commentaries about a book right before I start it. When I’m finished reading it I try to put my thoughts on paper before I read any other reviews or commentaries—so I won’t be overly influenced or intimidated by what others say.<br /><br />This book had me so confused I’ve read dozens of reviews and comments from other readers and still cannot articulate my own thoughts. I just don’t know what to think.<br /><br />Dark. Macabre. Coming of age story. Irish Huck Finn. Touching. Beautiful. Frightening. Twisted. Tragedy. <br /><br />Those are just some of the words others have used to describe “The Butcher Boy.” They all seem to fit. But none of them seem to capture the entirety of the book. In fact, even the combination of all of those words cannot explain the heart of this book. <br /><br />I wish I had read this as part of a lit course. Or with a reading group. (What a barrel of laughs that would be!) I wish I had not read it alone. I cannot quite wrap my brain around it. I am intrigued by Francie. I am furious at Francie. I am appalled by his actions. I understand his actions. I am mystified by his actions. I want to hug him. I want to flee from him. I want his character to rattle around in my brain as I try to come to terms with him. I want him out of my head NOW! I feel an almost overwhelming need to have a conversation with someone else who has read the book—just to try and sort out my thoughts. <br /><br />Maybe I cannot understand Francie (and how the story impacts me) because Francie does not really understand himself. He just is. And maybe my reaction to the book just is. Maybe it does not need to be articulated. Perhaps it should simply remain the confused jumble of thoughts it is. Much like Francie.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5658819664555110624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea" href="/user/show/7668412-andrea">Andrea</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668412-andrea?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668412-andrea?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer527161971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16393562951168240012">McCabe's impressive novel deserves credit for fearlessly tackling some very tricky literary territory. Francie Brady's lengthy, unbridled interior monologue is compounded with his increasing unreliability as a narrator.<br/><br/> You see, young Francie is going mad. His quickening psychosis is convincingly made apparent by his churning verbal output - as Brady's perception fragments, so does ours. His orange sky/frozen puddle/pigs/fish in the river imagery teams up and blends in so well with his obsessive</span>
  <span id="freeText16393562951168240012" style="display:none">McCabe's impressive novel deserves credit for fearlessly tackling some very tricky literary territory. Francie Brady's lengthy, unbridled interior monologue is compounded with his increasing unreliability as a narrator.<br /><br /> You see, young Francie is going mad. His quickening psychosis is convincingly made apparent by his churning verbal output - as Brady's perception fragments, so does ours. His orange sky/frozen puddle/pigs/fish in the river imagery teams up and blends in so well with his obsessive cogitating over past shames and humiliations that the reader has no choice but to go along and ride the erratic waves of a decomposing mind.<br /><br />I have to say that McCabe accomplishes quite a feat; the unruly, unpunctuated stream of consciousness aptly conveys rising disorder in Brady's mind, but it's also kind of a drag to read a couple hundred pages of it, from a most unreliable, yet only, narrator.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16393562951168240012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer323107729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15695329371043767244">This is a truly wonderful book. I read it in less than two days, devouring every word, dissecting the prose to extract the factual from the imagined from the dialogue. This was easy for me, although I can see how it might be difficult for readers not so familiar with Irish idiom (and the comic books of the sixties).<br/><br/>Other reviewers spoke about the violence in the book, but there is hardly any, and what there is occurs close to the end and is treated in a characteristically off-hand way.<br/><br/>I loved it</span>
  <span id="freeText15695329371043767244" style="display:none">This is a truly wonderful book. I read it in less than two days, devouring every word, dissecting the prose to extract the factual from the imagined from the dialogue. This was easy for me, although I can see how it might be difficult for readers not so familiar with Irish idiom (and the comic books of the sixties).<br /><br />Other reviewers spoke about the violence in the book, but there is hardly any, and what there is occurs close to the end and is treated in a characteristically off-hand way.<br /><br />I loved it. I loved the way everything in Francie's life was shown through his distorted perspective and yet could be seen by the reader with crystal precision.<br /><br />The book is a superb achievement, a shining example of what can be accomplished with words by a supreme artist.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15695329371043767244" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_144906169" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/144906169" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/144906169?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780880641470" href="/book/show/138969.The_Butcher_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=irish-lit">irish-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer144906169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8494595421926882900">Can children be evil? In literature this is certainly the case. I am reminded of the evil little girl, Rhoda Penmark, in <i>The Bad Seed</i> by William March. In Patrick McCabe's third novel we have a rival for Rhoda with Francie Brady. It is a journey into the heart of darkness: the mind of a desperately troubled kid one step away from madness and murder. Francie Brady is a schoolboy in a small town in Ireland. His father is a mean drunk and his mother a slovenly housekeeper, but Francie has a good bu</span>
  <span id="freeText8494595421926882900" style="display:none">Can children be evil? In literature this is certainly the case. I am reminded of the evil little girl, Rhoda Penmark, in <i>The Bad Seed</i> by William March. In Patrick McCabe's third novel we have a rival for Rhoda with Francie Brady. It is a journey into the heart of darkness: the mind of a desperately troubled kid one step away from madness and murder. Francie Brady is a schoolboy in a small town in Ireland. His father is a mean drunk and his mother a slovenly housekeeper, but Francie has a good buddy, Joe Purcell, and their Tom-and-Huck friendship is what sustains him. Then a seemingly trivial incident alters the landscape: Francie and Joe con the very proper Philip Nugent out of his prize collection of comic books, and Philip's mother calls the Bradys ``pigs.'' <br />Like many of Edgar Allan Poe's narrators, Francie will blame all his troubles on someone else, in his case Mrs. Nugent; it doesn't help that the Nugent household is a cozy haven, maddeningly out of his reach. Matters rapidly deteriorate. His mother enters a mental hospital. Francie runs away to Dublin; he returns to find that his ma, whom he had promised never to let down, has drowned herself. He breaks into the Nugents' house, defecates on the carpet, is sent to reform school, and (the unkindest cut) loses Joe to Philip Nugent. Francie tells us all of this in a voice that is the novel's greatest triumph--a minimally punctuated but always intelligible flow of razor-sharp impressions, name-calling, self-loathing, pop-culture detritus culled from comic books and John Wayne movies (the time is 1962), all delivered with the assurance of a stand-up comic. <br />We see in this story the longing for childhood innocence, now lost forever, and just an inkling of the gathering mental darkness that will lead to an inevitable denouement. Reminiscent of Salinger and Sillitoe, McCabe has created something all his own--an uncompromisingly bleak vision of a child who retains the pathos of a grubby urchin even as he evolves into a monster not unlike some of those that issue from Poe's imagination. His novel is a tour de force. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8494595421926882900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
