<div id="review_28156388" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28156388" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/787522-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1210278686p2/787522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28156388?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/787522-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780880641470" href="/book/show/138969.The_Butcher_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/787522-paul?shelf=2008">2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28156388" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14955745627039097319">Absolutely phenomenal. This is a must-read for anyone with a heart who can stand a bit of the old ultraviolence. It would be terribly reductive to describe this novel as a mashup of <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> and <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>, so I won't describe it as such, but its narrator definitely has that Holdenesque naivete and charm to him, and the language, while not as inventive as Burgess', is still a lot of fun. I kept telling people about the book as I was reading it and my descriptions all sounde</span>
  <span id="freeText14955745627039097319" style="display:none">Absolutely phenomenal. This is a must-read for anyone with a heart who can stand a bit of the old ultraviolence. It would be terribly reductive to describe this novel as a mashup of <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> and <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>, so I won't describe it as such, but its narrator definitely has that Holdenesque naivete and charm to him, and the language, while not as inventive as Burgess', is still a lot of fun. I kept telling people about the book as I was reading it and my descriptions all sounded incredibly sad. It's not a sad book, though, per se. Francie Brady has quite a sad lot dealt to him, sure, quite an unfortunate lot, but how he deals with it -- through a transparent, young-boy toughness and escapist imagination (McCabe uses this technique brilliantly, never as an escape mechanism himself; it's subtle and always appropriate) -- is where the book's humor and real pathos come from. Francie has this wonderful habit of saying what he wishes he could say and then immediately saying "No I didn't say that I said . . . " McCabe uses punctuation sparingly, and the book has a bit of a stream of consciousness feel to it, though it's completely accessible and an easy read. Still, at about 200 pages, it hardly feels short. Just incredibly well-crafted. Francie is funny as hell, but you wonder whether you'd consider him an absolute psychopath were his actions not filtered through his own voice/eyes. What a great, complex, and engaging character. His relationship to his friend Joe later on the novel is absolutely heartbreaking and true to life. A few small moments of literary transcendence here. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14955745627039097319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1139624894?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="gaudeo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="gaudeo" href="/user/show/2428663-gaudeo">gaudeo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1139624894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11617463573640654357">This book, a Booker Prize nominee, is disturbing but very enlightening. Set in Ireland, it follows the adolescence of Francis "Francie" Brady, the son of an alcoholic father and a mother literally at her wits' end. The family is poor, and I think the book's biggest strength is showing how poverty traps people from one generation to the next. But one cannot overlook Francie's main problem: he gets in trouble, and progressively more trouble as the story develops. McCabe does a great job of depicti</span>
  <span id="freeText11617463573640654357" style="display:none">This book, a Booker Prize nominee, is disturbing but very enlightening. Set in Ireland, it follows the adolescence of Francis "Francie" Brady, the son of an alcoholic father and a mother literally at her wits' end. The family is poor, and I think the book's biggest strength is showing how poverty traps people from one generation to the next. But one cannot overlook Francie's main problem: he gets in trouble, and progressively more trouble as the story develops. McCabe does a great job of depicting how people fear Francie, and how he toys with their fears. I won't reveal what happens, but it is unforgettable. This is a thought-provoking, haunting book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11617463573640654357" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText938789507182289147" style="display:none">I liked hearing the language in this book, i.e., the Irish accented English and turns of phrase. I listened to it on Audiobook which, of course, really brought those elements forward. Aside from that, though, I found the story to contain little more than caricatures of the Irish players that we've come to expect: the suffering Irish mother, the drunken Irish father, the predator-priest, to name a few. That said, the protagonist (a tween-age boy who wrestles with separating fantasy from reality) has a unique voice with an appealing quality of immediacy. I saw the movie too, which I actually thought was better than the book, FWIW.</span>

<div id="review_1246176224" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer14237459516547057866">This book made my skin crawl. The writing took some getting used to, McCabe is simply brilliant when it comes to messing with your head. This book is disturbing and made me uneasy almost every page. It is truly brilliant, however there were times when I struggled to understand if this was real or a conjoured up fantasy in Francie Brady's brain. It is for that reason alone that I give it 4 stars instead of 5. Well worth the Booker Prize and well worth your time, but I'm warning you, you won't sle</span>
  <span id="freeText14237459516547057866" style="display:none">This book made my skin crawl. The writing took some getting used to, McCabe is simply brilliant when it comes to messing with your head. This book is disturbing and made me uneasy almost every page. It is truly brilliant, however there were times when I struggled to understand if this was real or a conjoured up fantasy in Francie Brady's brain. It is for that reason alone that I give it 4 stars instead of 5. Well worth the Booker Prize and well worth your time, but I'm warning you, you won't sleep easy!</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('nSZxdizw+3xp5VnuQDVL7daAbyaI2+aT1VhW1AMwoTiy64MFPrNrxo0fps9Dl+Kim6wKHAODjuAWkgoVQDQobQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/47391.Patrick_McCabe">About Patrick McCabe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/47391.Patrick_McCabe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1278858960p3/47391.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/47391.Patrick_McCabe">Patrick McCabe
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
189
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3859488662259595259">Patrick McCabe came to prominence with the publication of his third adult novel, <i>The Butcher Boy</i>, in 1992; the book was shortlisted for the Booker Prize in Britain and won the Irish Times-Aer Lingus Prize for fiction. McCabe's strength as an author lies in his ability to probe behind the veneer of respectability and conformity to reveal the brutality and the cloying and corrupting stagnation of Ir</span>
  <span id="freeText3859488662259595259" style="display:none">Patrick McCabe came to prominence with the publication of his third adult novel, <i>The Butcher Boy</i>, in 1992; the book was shortlisted for the Booker Prize in Britain and won the Irish Times-Aer Lingus Prize for fiction. McCabe's strength as an author lies in his ability to probe behind the veneer of respectability and conformity to reveal the brutality and the cloying and corrupting stagnation of Irish small-town life, but he is able to find compassion for the subjects of his fiction. His prose has a vitality and an anti-authoritarian bent, using everyday language to deconstruct the ideologies at work in Ireland between the early 1960s and the late 1970s. His books can be read as a plea for a pluralistic Irish culture that can encompass the past without being dominated by it.<br /><br />McCabe is an Irish writer of mostly dark and violent novels of contemporary, often small-town, Ireland. His novels include <i>The Butcher Boy</i> (1992) and <i>Breakfast on Pluto</i> (1998), both shortlisted for the Booker Prize. He has also written a children's book (<i>The Adventures of Shay Mouse</i>) and several radio plays broadcast by the RTÉ and the BBC Radio 4. <i>The Butcher Boy</i> and <i>Breakfast on Pluto</i> have both been adapted into films by Irish director Neil Jordan.<br /><br />McCabe lives in Clones, Co. Monaghan with his wife and two daughters.<br /><br />Pat McCabe is also credited with having invented the "Bog Gothic" genre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3859488662259595259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
